<a href="/wiki/Delta_Cryogenic_Second_Stage" title="Delta Cryogenic Second Stage">ICPS</a></th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Length</th><td>13.7&#160;m (44&#160;ft 11&#160;in)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Diameter</th><td>5&#160;m (16&#160;ft 5&#160;in)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Empty mass</th><td>3,490&#160;kg (7,690&#160;lb)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Gross mass</th><td>30,710&#160;kg (67,700&#160;lb)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Engines</th><td>1 <a href="/wiki/RL10" title="RL10">RL10B-2</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Thrust</th><td>110.1&#160;kN (24,800&#160;lbf)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Specific_impulse" title="Specific impulse">Specific impulse</a></th><td>462 seconds (4.53&#160;km/s)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Burn time</th><td>1125&#160;seconds</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Fuel</th><td><a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">LH<sub>2</sub></a> / <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">LOX</a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background: #ddf;">Second stage (Block 1B, Block 2) &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Exploration_Upper_Stage" title="Exploration Upper Stage">Exploration Upper Stage</a></th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Diameter</th><td>8.4&#160;m (27&#160;ft 7&#160;in)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Engines</th><td>4 <a href="/wiki/RL10" title="RL10">RL10</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Thrust</th><td>99,000&#160;lbf (440&#160;kN)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Fuel</th><td><a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">LH<sub>2</sub></a> / <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">LOX</a></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><th colspan="2">\n</th></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Space Launch System</b> (<b>SLS</b>) is an American <a href="/wiki/Shuttle-Derived_Launch_Vehicle" title="Shuttle-Derived Launch Vehicle">Space Shuttle-derived</a> <a href="/wiki/Super_heavy-lift_launch_vehicle" title="Super heavy-lift launch vehicle">super heavy-lift</a> <a href="/wiki/Expendable_launch_system" title="Expendable launch system">expendable launch vehicle</a>. It is part of <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>\'s deep space exploration plans<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> including a crewed mission to Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf-20170406_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-nsf-20170406-12">[12]</a></sup> SLS follows the cancellation of the <a href="/wiki/Constellation_program" title="Constellation program">Constellation program</a>, and is to replace the retired <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a>. The <a href="/wiki/NASA_Authorization_Act_of_2010" title="NASA Authorization Act of 2010">NASA Authorization Act of 2010</a> envisions the transformation of the Constellation program\'s <a href="/wiki/Ares_I" title="Ares I">Ares I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ares_V" title="Ares V">Ares V</a> vehicle designs into a single launch vehicle usable for both crew and cargo, similar to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ares_IV" title="Ares IV">Ares IV</a> concept. The SLS is to be the most powerful rocket ever built<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> with a total thrust greater than that of the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> although Saturn V could carry a greater payload mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SaturnV_compar_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-SaturnV_compar-15">[N 1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SV_payld_cap_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-SV_payld_cap-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnbc.com_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnbc.com-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The SLS launch vehicle is to be upgraded over time with more powerful versions. Its initial Block 1 version is to lift a <a href="/wiki/Payload" title="Payload">payload</a> of 95 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_ton" title="Metric ton">metric tons</a> to <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> (LEO), which will be increased with the debut of Block 1B and the <a href="/wiki/Exploration_Upper_Stage" title="Exploration Upper Stage">Exploration Upper Stage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> Block 2 will replace the initial Shuttle-derived boosters with advanced boosters and is planned to have a LEO capability of more than 130 metric tons to meet the congressional requirement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AuthAct10_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-AuthAct10-20">[19]</a></sup> These upgrades will allow the SLS to lift astronauts and hardware to destinations beyond LEO: on a <a href="/wiki/Circumlunar_trajectory" title="Circumlunar trajectory">circumlunar trajectory</a> as part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploration_Mission_1" title="Exploration Mission 1">Exploration Mission 1</a> &amp; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploration_Mission_2" title="Exploration Mission 2">2</a> with Block 1; to deliver elements of the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Orbital_Platform-Gateway" title="Lunar Orbital Platform-Gateway">Lunar Orbital Platform-Gateway</a> (LOP-G) with Block 1B; and to <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> with Block 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf-20170406_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-nsf-20170406-12">[12]</a></sup> The SLS will launch the <a href="/wiki/Orion_(spacecraft)#Design" title="Orion (spacecraft)">Orion Crew and Service Module</a> and may support trips to the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> if necessary. SLS will use the ground operations and launch facilities at NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a>, Florida.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Design_and_development"><span class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Boosters"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Boosters</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-5"><a class="toctext">Exploration Upper Stage</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-10"><a href="#Other_upper_stages"><span class="tocnumber">2.3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Other upper stages</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Payload_carrying_capacity"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Payload carrying capacity</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Fabrication_and_testing"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Fabrication and testing</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Program_costs_and_funding"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Program costs and funding</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Funding_history_and_planning"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Funding history and planning</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Criticism"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Criticism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Missions"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Missions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Gallery"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Gallery</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design_and_development">Design and development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Design and development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SLS_Evolved_Configurations.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="253" data-file-width="395" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4d/SLS_Evolved_Configurations.png/220px-SLS_Evolved_Configurations.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4d/SLS_Evolved_Configurations.png/330px-SLS_Evolved_Configurations.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/4d/SLS_Evolved_Configurations.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SLS_Evolved_Configurations.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Space Launch System\'s planned upgrade path</div></div></div>\n<p>On September 14, 2011, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> announced its design selection for the new launch system, declaring that it, in combination with the <a href="/wiki/Orion_(spacecraft)" title="Orion (spacecraft)">Orion spacecraft</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPCVDecision_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-MPCVDecision-21">[20]</a></sup> would take the agency\'s astronauts farther into space than ever before and provide the cornerstone for future US human space exploration efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SLSAnnounce_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-SLSAnnounce-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Briefing_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Briefing-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-24">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the early development of the SLS a number of configurations were considered, including a Block 0 variant with three main engines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFTrades_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSFTrades-25">[24]</a></sup> a Block 1A variant that would have upgraded the vehicle\'s boosters instead of its second stage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFTrades_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-NSFTrades-25">[24]</a></sup> and a Block 2 with five main engines and a different second stage, the <a href="/wiki/Earth_Departure_Stage" title="Earth Departure Stage">Earth Departure Stage</a>, with up to three <a href="/wiki/J-2X" title="J-2X">J-2X</a> engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf-20120223_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-nsf-20120223-26">[25]</a></sup>  In February 2015, it was reported that NASA evaluations showed "over performance" versus the baseline payload for Block 1 and Block 1B configurations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFAdvBooster022015_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSFAdvBooster022015-27">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Three versions of the SLS launch vehicle are planned: Block 1, Block 1B, and Block 2. Each will use the same core stage with four main engines, but Block 1B will feature a more powerful second stage called the <a href="/wiki/Exploration_Upper_Stage" title="Exploration Upper Stage">Exploration Upper Stage</a> (EUS), and Block 2 will combine the EUS with upgraded boosters. Block 1 has a baseline LEO payload capacity of 95 metric tons (105 short tons) and Block 1B has a baseline of 105 metric tons (116 short tons).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slsfact-20171011_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-slsfact-20171011-28">[27]</a></sup> The proposed Block 2 will have lift capacity of 130 metric tons (140 short tons), which is similar to that of the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AuthAct10_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-AuthAct10-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>  Some sources state this would make the SLS the most capable <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_orbital_launch_systems" title="Comparison of orbital launch systems">heavy lift vehicle</a> built;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spaceflightnow_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-spaceflightnow-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> although the Saturn V lifted approximately 140 metric tons (150 short tons) to LEO in the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">Apollo 17</a> mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SV_payld_cap_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-SV_payld_cap-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-America_Threshold_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-America_Threshold-32">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On July 31, 2013, the SLS passed the Preliminary Design Review (PDR). The review encompassed all aspects of the SLS\'s design, not only the rocket and boosters but also ground support and logistical arrangements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> On August 7, 2014 the SLS Block 1 passed a milestone known as Key Decision Point C and entered full-scale development, with an estimated launch date of November 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Review_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Review-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SNDebut082014_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-SNDebut082014-35">[34]</a></sup> In April 2017, NASA announced that the schedule for the maiden flight would slip to 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2019-slip_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-2019-slip-36">[35]</a></sup> In November 2017, the EM-1 maiden flight slipped further to June 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2020-slip_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-2020-slip-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vehicle_description">Vehicle description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Vehicle description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orange_tank_SLS_launch_through_clouds_-_Post_CDR.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2160" data-file-width="3042" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Orange_tank_SLS_launch_through_clouds_-_Post_CDR.jpg/280px-Orange_tank_SLS_launch_through_clouds_-_Post_CDR.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Orange_tank_SLS_launch_through_clouds_-_Post_CDR.jpg/420px-Orange_tank_SLS_launch_through_clouds_-_Post_CDR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Orange_tank_SLS_launch_through_clouds_-_Post_CDR.jpg/560px-Orange_tank_SLS_launch_through_clouds_-_Post_CDR.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orange_tank_SLS_launch_through_clouds_-_Post_CDR.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s rendering of a SLS Block 1</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Core_Stage">Core Stage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Core Stage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Space Launch System\'s Core Stage will be 8.4 meters (28&#160;ft) in diameter and use four <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_main_engine" title="Space Shuttle main engine">RS-25</a> engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Fact-Sht_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Fact-Sht-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFTrades_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-NSFTrades-25">[24]</a></sup> Initial flights will use modified RS-25D engines left over from the Space Shuttle program;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> later flights are expected to switch to a cheaper version of the engine not intended for reuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFStennis012015_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSFStennis012015-38">[37]</a></sup> The stage\'s structure will consist of a modified <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_external_tank" title="Space Shuttle external tank">Space Shuttle external tank</a> with the aft section adapted to accept the rocket\'s Main Propulsion System (MPS) and the top converted to host an interstage structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spaceflightnow_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-spaceflightnow-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFAnnounce_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSFAnnounce-39">[38]</a></sup> It will be fabricated at the <a href="/wiki/Michoud_Assembly_Facility" title="Michoud Assembly Facility">Michoud Assembly Facility</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The core stage will be common across all currently planned evolutions of the SLS. Initial planning included studies of a smaller Block 0 configuration with three RS-25 engines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFDual_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSFDual-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> which was eliminated to avoid the need to substantially redesign the core stage for more powerful variants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFTrades_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-NSFTrades-25">[24]</a></sup> Likewise, while early Block 2 plans included five RS-25 engines on the core,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf-20120223_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-nsf-20120223-26">[25]</a></sup> it was later baselined with four engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFAdvBooster022015_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-NSFAdvBooster022015-27">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boosters">Boosters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Boosters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb href="/wiki/File:Space_Launch_System_Booster_Passes_Major_Ground_Test.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>SLS Booster test in the <a href="/wiki/Utah#Climate" title="Utah">Utah desert</a></div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Shuttle-derived_boosters">Shuttle-derived boosters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Shuttle-derived boosters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Blocks 1 and 1B of the SLS will use two <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Solid_Rocket_Booster#Five-segment_booster" title="Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster">five-segment Solid Rocket Boosters</a> (SRBs), which are based on the four-segment <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Solid_Rocket_Booster" title="Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster">Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Boosters</a>. Modifications for the SLS included the addition of a center booster segment, new avionics, and new insulation which eliminates the Shuttle SRB\'s asbestos and is 860&#160;kg (1,900&#160;lb) lighter. The five-segment SRBs provide approximately 25% more total impulse than the Shuttle SRB and will not be recovered after use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFHowToLaunch022012_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSFHowToLaunch022012-44">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_ATK" title="Orbital ATK">Orbital ATK</a> (formerly <a href="/wiki/Alliant_Techsystems" title="Alliant Techsystems">Alliant Techsystems</a>, part of <a href="/wiki/Northrop_Grumman" title="Northrop Grumman">Northrop Grumman</a> since mid-2018) has completed full-duration static fire tests of five-segment SRBs.  These include successful firings of three developmental motors (DM-1 to DM-3) from 2009 to 2011. The DM-2 motor was cooled to a core temperature of 40&#160;&#176;F (4&#160;&#176;C), and DM-3 was heated to above 90&#160;&#176;F (32&#160;&#176;C) to validate performance at extreme temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> Qualification Motor 1 (QM-1) was tested on March 10, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFQM032015_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSFQM032015-48">[47]</a></sup> Qualification Motor 2 was successfully tested on June 28, 2016. It was the final ground test before Exploration Mission 1 (EM-1).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Advanced_boosters">Advanced boosters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Advanced boosters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>For Block 2, NASA plans to switch from Shuttle-derived five-segment SRBs to advanced boosters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spacerefSept14_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-spacerefSept14-49">[48]</a></sup> This will occur after development of the Exploration Upper Stage for Block 1B. Early plans would have developed advanced boosters before an updated second stage; this configuration was called Block 1A. By 2012 NASA planned to select these new boosters through an Advanced Booster Competition which was to be held in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Fact-Sht_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Fact-Sht-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> Several companies proposed boosters for this competition:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Aerojet" title="Aerojet">Aerojet</a>, in partnership with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teledyne" title="Teledyne">Teledyne Brown</a>, offered a booster powered by three AJ1E6 engines, which would be a newly developed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LOX" title="LOX">LOX</a> / <a href="/wiki/RP-1" title="RP-1">RP-1</a> <a href="/wiki/Staged_combustion_cycle" title="Staged combustion cycle">oxidizer-rich staged combustion</a> engine. Each AJ1E6 engine would produce 4,900&#160;kN (1,100,000&#160;lbf) thrust using a single turbopump to supply dual combustion chambers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> On February 14, 2013, NASA awarded Aerojet a $23.3 million, 30-month contract to build a 2,400&#160;kN (550,000&#160;lbf) main injector and thrust chamber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasaFinalAdvancedBoosterContract_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasaFinalAdvancedBoosterContract-52">[51]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Alliant_Techsystems" title="Alliant Techsystems">Alliant Techsystems</a> (ATK) proposed an advanced SRB nicknamed "Dark Knight". This booster would switch from a steel case to one made of lighter composite material, use a more energetic propellant, and reduce the number of segments from five to four.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-:32-53">[52]</a></sup> It would deliver over 20,000&#160;kN (4,500,000&#160;lbf) maximum thrust and weigh 790,000&#160;kg (1,750,000&#160;lb) at ignition. According to ATK, the advanced booster would be 40% less expensive than the Shuttle-derived five-segment SRB. It is uncertain if the booster will allow SLS to deliver the mandated 130&#160;t to LEO without the addition of a fifth engine to the core stage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFAdvBooster022015_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-NSFAdvBooster022015-27">[26]</a></sup> as a 2013 analysis indicated a maximum capacity of 113&#160;t with the baselined four-engine core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney_Rocketdyne" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney Rocketdyne">Pratt &amp; Whitney Rocketdyne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dynetics" title="Dynetics">Dynetics</a> proposed a liquid-fueled booster named "<a href="/wiki/Saturn_C-3#Pyrios" title="Saturn C-3">Pyrios</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> Each booster would use two <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_F-1#F-1B_booster" title="Rocketdyne F-1">F-1B</a> engines which together would deliver a maximum thrust of 16,000&#160;kN (3,600,000&#160;lbf) total, and be able to continuously throttle down to a minimum of 12,000&#160;kN (2,600,000&#160;lbf). The F-1B would be derived from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-1_(rocket_engine)" title="F-1 (rocket engine)">F-1 engine</a>, which powered the <a href="/wiki/S-IC" title="S-IC">first stage</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a>. It would have been easier to assemble, with fewer parts and a simplified design,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> while providing improved efficiency and a thrust increase of 110&#160;kN (25,000&#160;lbf).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> Estimates in 2012 indicated that the Pyrios booster could increase Block 2 low-Earth orbit payload to 150&#160;t, 20&#160;t more than the baseline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Christopher Crumbly, manager of NASA\'s SLS advanced development office in January 2013 commented on the booster competition, "The F-1 has great advantages because it is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas-generator_cycle_(rocket)" title="Gas-generator cycle (rocket)">gas generator</a> and has a very simple cycle. The oxygen-rich <a href="/wiki/Staged_combustion_cycle" title="Staged combustion cycle">staged combustion cycle</a> [Aerojet\'s engine] has great advantages because it has a higher <a href="/wiki/Specific_impulse" title="Specific impulse">specific impulse</a>. The Russians have been flying ox[ygen]-rich for a long time. Either one can work. The solids [of ATK] can work."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spacereview_SLS_BII_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-spacereview_SLS_BII-59">[58]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Later analysis showed the Block 1A configuration would result in high acceleration which would be unsuitable for Orion and could require a costly redesign of the Block 1 core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFWindTunnel1B072012_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSFWindTunnel1B072012-60">[59]</a></sup> In 2014, NASA confirmed the development of Block 1B instead of Block 1A and called off the 2015 booster competition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFAdvBooster022015_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-NSFAdvBooster022015-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> In February 2015, it was reported that SLS is expected to fly with the five-segment SRB until at least the late 2020s, and modifications to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LC-39B" title="LC-39B">Launch Pad 39B</a>, its flame trench, and SLS\'s <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Launcher_Platform" title="Mobile Launcher Platform">Mobile Launcher Platform</a> were evaluated based on SLS launching with solid-fuel boosters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFAdvBooster022015_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-NSFAdvBooster022015-27">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Upper_Stage">Upper Stage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Upper Stage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Common_Extensible_Cryogenic_Engine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3869" data-file-width="2591" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Common_Extensible_Cryogenic_Engine.jpg/170px-Common_Extensible_Cryogenic_Engine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Common_Extensible_Cryogenic_Engine.jpg/255px-Common_Extensible_Cryogenic_Engine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Common_Extensible_Cryogenic_Engine.jpg/340px-Common_Extensible_Cryogenic_Engine.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Common_Extensible_Cryogenic_Engine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/RL10" title="RL10">RL10</a> engine, like the one pictured above, will be used as the <a href="/wiki/Multistage_rocket" title="Multistage rocket">second stage</a> engine in both the ICPS and EUS upper stages.</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Interim_Cryogenic_Propulsion_Stage">Interim Cryogenic Propulsion Stage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Interim Cryogenic Propulsion Stage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Block 1, scheduled to fly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploration_Mission_1" title="Exploration Mission 1">Exploration Mission 1</a> (EM-1) in 2020,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2020-slip_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-2020-slip-6">[6]</a></sup> will use the Interim Cryogenic Propulsion Stage (ICPS). This stage will be a modified <a href="/wiki/Delta_IV" title="Delta IV">Delta IV</a> 5&#8211;meter <a href="/wiki/Delta_Cryogenic_Second_Stage" title="Delta Cryogenic Second Stage">Delta Cryogenic Second Stage</a> (DCSS),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FI_DCSS_selected_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FI_DCSS_selected-62">[61]</a></sup> and will be powered by a single <a href="/wiki/RL10" title="RL10">RL10B-2</a>. Block 1 will be capable of lifting 95&#160;t in this configuration, however the ICPS will be considered part of the payload and be placed into an initial 1,800&#160;km by -93&#160;km <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suborbital" title="Suborbital">suborbital</a> trajectory to ensure safe disposal of the core stage. ICPS will perform an orbital insertion burn at apogee, and then a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Translunar_injection" title="Translunar injection">translunar injection</a> burn to send the uncrewed Orion on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circumlunar" title="Circumlunar">circumlunar</a> excursion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup>  In May 2018, NASA updated the payload capability of the SLS Block 1 from 70 to 95 metric tons to <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_2018_Update_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_2018_Update-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration_Upper_Stage">Exploration Upper Stage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Exploration Upper Stage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Exploration_Upper_Stage" title="Exploration Upper Stage">Exploration Upper Stage</a> (EUS) was scheduled to fly on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploration_Mission_2" title="Exploration Mission 2">Exploration Mission 2</a> (EM-2). It was expected to be used by Block 1B and Block 2 and, like the core stage, have a diameter of 8.4 meters. The EUS is to be powered by four RL10 engines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFEUS_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSFEUS-64">[63]</a></sup> complete the SLS ascent phase and then re-ignite to send its payload to destinations beyond low-Earth orbit, similar to the role performed by the Saturn V\'s 3rd stage, the <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_J-2" title="Rocketdyne J-2">J-2</a> powered <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFPDR032015_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSFPDR032015-65">[64]</a></sup> Because of delays in building the mobile launch platform needed to hold the more powerful EUS, the EM-2 flight might launch earlier than planned but it will not use the EUS,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> it will not carry a module for the Lunar Gateway and it will not orbit the Moon.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_upper_stages">Other upper stages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Other upper stages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1013" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg/280px-Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg/420px-Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg/560px-Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s impression of the Bimodal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_Thermal_Rocket" title="Nuclear Thermal Rocket">Nuclear Thermal Rocket</a> engines on the Mars Transfer Vehicle (MTV). Cold launched, it would be assembled in-orbit by a number of Block 2 SLS payload lifts. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orion_spacecraft" title="Orion spacecraft">Orion spacecraft</a> is docked on the left.</div></div></div>\n<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Earth_Departure_Stage" title="Earth Departure Stage">Earth Departure Stage</a>, powered by <a href="/wiki/J-2X" title="J-2X">J-2X</a> engines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> was to be the upper stage of the Block 2 SLS had NASA decided to develop Block 1A instead of Block 1B and the EUS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFPDR032015_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-NSFPDR032015-65">[64]</a></sup></li>\n<li>In 2013, NASA and <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> analyzed the performance of several second stage options. The analysis was based on a second stage usable propellant load of 105 metric tons, except for the Block 1 and ICPS, which will carry 27.1 metric tons.  The ICPS upper stage and upper stages using four RL10 engines and two <a href="/wiki/RL60" title="RL60">MB60/RL60</a> engines and one <a href="/wiki/J-2X" title="J-2X">J-2X</a> engine were studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsfipdo_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-nsfipdo-69">[68]</a></sup>  In 2014, NASA also considered using the European <a href="/wiki/Vinci_(rocket_engine)" title="Vinci (rocket engine)">Vinci</a> instead of the RL10. The Vinci offers the same specific impulse but with 64% greater thrust, which would allow for a reduction of one or two of the four second stage engines for the same performance for a lower cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> Robotic exploration <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)#Future_missions" title="Europa (moon)">missions to Jupiter\'s water-ice moon Europa</a> are increasingly seen as well suited to the lift capabilities of the Block 1B SLS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup></li>\n<li>An additional beyond-LEO engine for <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_spaceflight" title="Interplanetary spaceflight">interplanetary travel</a> from Earth orbit to Mars orbit, and back, is being studied as of 2013 at <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">Marshall Space Flight Center</a> with a focus on <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_thermal_rocket" title="Nuclear thermal rocket">nuclear thermal rocket</a> (NTR) engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> In historical ground testing, NTRs proved to be at least <a href="/wiki/Specific_impulse" title="Specific impulse">twice as efficient</a> as the most advanced chemical engines, which would allow quicker transfer time and increased cargo capacity. The shorter flight duration, estimated at 3&#8211;4 months with NTR engines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> compared to 6&#8211;9 months using chemical engines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> would reduce crew exposure to potentially harmful and difficult to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiation_shielding" title="Radiation shielding">shield</a> <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arc.aiaa.org_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-arc.aiaa.org-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NTP2012_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-NTP2012-78">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> NTR engines, such as the <a href="/wiki/NERVA" title="NERVA">Pewee</a> of <a href="/wiki/Project_Rover" title="Project Rover">Project Rover</a>, were selected in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mars_Semi-Direct" title="Mars Semi-Direct">Mars Design Reference Architecture</a> (DRA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arc.aiaa.org_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-arc.aiaa.org-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NTP2012_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-NTP2012-78">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarsRoadmap_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-MarsRoadmap-80">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> In 2017 NASA continued research and development on NTRs, designing for space applications with civilian approved materials, with a three-year, $18.8-million contract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NTP-2017-08_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-NTP-2017-08-82">[81]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Payload_carrying_capacity">Payload carrying capacity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Payload carrying capacity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a style="display:none">7002130000000000000&#9824;</span>130 t<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-creech-2014_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-creech-2014-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001450000000000000&#9824;</span>45 t<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_2018_Update_4-8"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_2018_Update-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fabrication_and_testing">Fabrication and testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fabrication and testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SLS_on_MLP_at_night.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2211" data-file-width="1195" decoding="async" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/SLS_on_MLP_at_night.jpg/170px-SLS_on_MLP_at_night.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/SLS_on_MLP_at_night.jpg/255px-SLS_on_MLP_at_night.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/SLS_on_MLP_at_night.jpg/340px-SLS_on_MLP_at_night.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SLS_on_MLP_at_night.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rendering of the SLS Block 1 with its older black-and-white paint scheme, showing core stage, two 5-segment SRBs, and the smaller upper stage.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SLS_Liquid_Oxygen_Tank_Hardware_in_Welding.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1560" data-file-width="1041" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/SLS_Liquid_Oxygen_Tank_Hardware_in_Welding.jpg/220px-SLS_Liquid_Oxygen_Tank_Hardware_in_Welding.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/SLS_Liquid_Oxygen_Tank_Hardware_in_Welding.jpg/330px-SLS_Liquid_Oxygen_Tank_Hardware_in_Welding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/SLS_Liquid_Oxygen_Tank_Hardware_in_Welding.jpg/440px-SLS_Liquid_Oxygen_Tank_Hardware_in_Welding.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SLS_Liquid_Oxygen_Tank_Hardware_in_Welding.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Welding of the SLS liquid oxygen tank in the South Vertical Assembly Building at <a href="/wiki/Michoud_Assembly_Facility" title="Michoud Assembly Facility">Michoud Assembly Facility</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In mid-November 2014, construction of the first SLS rocket began using the new welding system in the South Vertical Assembly Building at NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Michoud_Assembly_Facility" title="Michoud Assembly Facility">Michoud Assembly Facility</a>, where the Core Stage will be assembled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The SLS will have the ability to tolerate a minimum of 13 tanking cycles due to launch scrubs and other launch delays before launch. The assembled rocket is to be able to remain at the launch pad for a minimum of 180 days and can remain in stacked configuration for at least 200 days without destacking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 2015, NASA began test firing RS-25 engines in preparation for use on SLS. Tests continued throughout Spring of 2015. Further testing was conducted in 2016 and 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSFStennis012015_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-NSFStennis012015-38">[37]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Multiple facilities throughout the country have started full scale fabrication of different segments of the launch vehicle. Orbital ATK began casting propellant for the solid rocket boosters and manufacturing parts for the boosters in 2016. The company test fired a solid rocket booster in early 2015,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> and a second booster in June 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Confidence article builds for the core stage began on January 5, 2016 and were expected to be completed in late January of that year. Once completed the test articles will be sent to ensure structural integrity at Marshall Spaceflight Center. The ICPS for EM-1 was slated for assembly in late January 2016, and a structural test article was delivered to NASA in 2015 for confidence testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SlSUpdate2016_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-SlSUpdate2016-87">[86]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The text near this tag is dated. (February 2016)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Program_costs_and_funding">Program costs and funding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Program costs and funding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>During the joint Senate-NASA presentation in September 2011, it was stated that the SLS program had a projected development cost of $18 billion through 2017, with $10 billion for the SLS rocket, $6 billion for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orion_Multi-Purpose_Crew_Vehicle" title="Orion Multi-Purpose Crew Vehicle">Orion Multi-Purpose Crew Vehicle</a> and $2 billion for upgrades to the launch pad and other facilities at Kennedy Space Center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Costs_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Costs-88">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cspan_sept_2011_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cspan_sept_2011-89">[88]</a></sup> These costs and schedule were considered optimistic in an independent 2011 cost assessment report by <a href="/wiki/Booz_Allen_Hamilton" title="Booz Allen Hamilton">Booz Allen Hamilton</a> for NASA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> An unofficial 2011 NASA document estimated the cost of the program through 2025 to total at least $41bn for four 95&#160;t launches (1 uncrewed, 3 crewed),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj_sticker_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-wsj_sticker-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESDBudget_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-ESDBudget-3">[3]</a></sup> with the 130&#160;t version ready no earlier than 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Costs2_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Costs2-91">[90]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Human Exploration Framework Team (HEFT) estimated unit costs for Block 0 at $1.6bn and Block 1 at $1.86bn in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HEFT_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-HEFT-92">[91]</a></sup> However, since these estimates were made the Block 0 SLS vehicle was dropped in late 2011, and the design was not completed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> The Space Review estimated the cost per launch at $5 billion, depending on the rate of launches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> NASA announced in 2013 that the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> will build the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orion_Service_Module" title="Orion Service Module">Orion Service Module</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>NASA SLS deputy project manager Jody Singer at Marshall Space Flight Center, Huntsville, Alabama stated in September 2012 that $500 million per launch is a reasonable target cost for SLS, with a relatively minor dependence of costs on launch capability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSNBC_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC-97">[96]</a></sup>  By comparison, a <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a> launch cost US$185 to US$189 million in 1969-1971 dollars or roughly $1.23 billion in 2016 dollars adjusted for inflation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SP4221Ch6_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-SP4221Ch6-98">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On July 24, 2014, Government Accountability Office audit predicted that SLS would not launch by the end of 2017 as originally planned since NASA had not been receiving sufficient funding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In August 2014, as the SLS program passed its Key Decision Point C review and entered full development, costs from February 2014 until its planned launch in September 2018 were estimated at $7.021 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SNDebut082014_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-SNDebut082014-35">[34]</a></sup> Ground systems modifications and construction would require an additional $1.8 billion over the same time period. As of  February 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the Orion spacecraft was expected to enter its Key Decision Point C review in the first half of 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For Fiscal Year 2015, NASA received an appropriation of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$1.7 billion</span> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Congress" title="US Congress">Congress</a> for SLS, an amount that was approximately <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$320 million</span> greater than the amount requested by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obama_administration" title="Obama administration">Obama administration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfn20141214_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-sfn20141214-102">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2018, NASA\'s inspector general reported that the Boeing SLS stages contract portion (accounting "for over 40 percent of the $11.9 billion spent on the SLS Program" as of August 2018) is expected to cost a total of US$8.9 billion by 2021, which is twice the initial planned amount.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Funding_history_and_planning">Funding history and planning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Funding history and planning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>For fiscal years 2011 through 2018, the SLS program had expended funding totaling $13,999 million in nominal dollars. This is equivalent to $15,109 million adjusting to 2018 dollars using the NASA New Start Inflation Indices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Fiscal Year\n</th>\n<th>Funding ($millions)\n</th>\n<th>Line Item Name\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2011\n</td>\n<td>$1,536.1\n</td>\n<td>Actuals, 2011, Space Launch System<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> <br/>(Formal SLS Program reporting excludes the Fiscal 2011 budget as being before "formulation start" in November 2011,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> Fiscal Year 2012)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2012\n</td>\n<td>$1,497.5\n</td>\n<td>Actuals, 2012, Space Launch System<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2013\n</td>\n<td>$1,414.9\n</td>\n<td>Actuals, 2013, Space Launch System<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2014\n</td>\n<td>$1,600.0\n</td>\n<td>Actuals, 2014, Space Launch System<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FY2016_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FY2016-109">[108]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2015\n</td>\n<td>$1,678.6\n</td>\n<td>Actuals, 2015, Space Launch System<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FY2017_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FY2017-110">[109]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2016\n</td>\n<td>$1,971.9\n</td>\n<td>Enacted, 2016, Space Launch System<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FY2017_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-FY2017-110">[109]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2017\n</td>\n<td>$2,150.0\n</td>\n<td>Appropriated, 2017, Space Launch System<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-111">[110]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2018\n</td>\n<td>$2,150.0\n</td>\n<td>Appropriated, 2018, Space Launch System<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-112">[111]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2011-2018\n</td>\n<td>Total: $13,999M\n</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Excluded from the prior SLS costs are:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Costs of the predecessor <a href="/wiki/Ares_V" title="Ares V">Ares V / Cargo Launch Vehicle</a> (funded from 2008 to 2010)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-113">[112]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Costs for the <a href="/wiki/Ares_I" title="Ares I">Ares 1 / Crew Launch Vehicle</a> (funded from 2006 to 2010, a total of $4.8 billion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> in development that included the 5-segment Solid Rocket Boosters that will be used on the SLS)</li>\n<li>Costs to assemble, integrate, prepare and launch the SLS and its payloads such as Orion (funded under the NASA Ground Operations Project,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> currently about $400M<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FY2016_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-FY2016-109">[108]</a></sup> per year)</li>\n<li>Costs of payloads for the SLS (such as <a href="/wiki/Orion_(spacecraft)" title="Orion (spacecraft)">Orion</a>)</li></ul>\n<p>Included in the prior SLS costs are:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Costs of the interim Upper Stage for the SLS, the Interim Cryogenic Propulsion Stage (ICPS) for SLS, which includes a $412M contract<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Costs of the final Upper Stage for the SLS, the Exploration Upper Stage (<a href="/wiki/Exploration_Upper_Stage" title="Exploration Upper Stage">EUS</a>) (funded at $85M in 2016,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> $300M in 2017<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-111">[110]</a></sup> and $300M in 2018<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-112">[111]</a></sup>)</li></ul>\n<p>For 2019 to 2023, NASA "notional"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup> yearly budgets for SLS range from $2.1 to $2.3B a year. As of late 2015, the SLS program has a 70% confidence level for its "first Orion mission with astronauts by 2023" according to the Associate Administrator for NASA, Robert Lightfoot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As of mid-2018, the SLS is scheduling its first test launch, with no crew, for mid-2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are no NASA estimates for the SLS program recurring yearly costs once operational, for a certain flight rate per year, or for the resulting average costs per flight. Bill Hill, NASA manager of exploration systems development has indicated "My top number for Orion, SLS, and the ground systems that support it is $2 billion or less" (annually).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> NASA associate administrator William H. Gerstenmaier has indicated, "[per mission] costs must be derived from the data and are not directly available. This was done by design to lower NASA\'s expenditures."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Launch_System&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Space_Access_Society" title="Space Access Society">Space Access Society</a>, <a href="/wiki/Space_Frontier_Foundation" title="Space Frontier Foundation">Space Frontier Foundation</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Planetary_Society" title="The Planetary Society">The Planetary Society</a> called for cancellation of the project in 2011&#8211;12, arguing that SLS will consume the funds for other projects from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NASA_budget" title="NASA budget">NASA budget</a> and will not reduce <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_orbital_launch_systems" title="Comparison of orbital launch systems">launch costs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SASRelease_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-SASRelease-125">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFF_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-SFF-126">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TPSStatement_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-TPSStatement-127">[126]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">U.S. Representative</a> <a href="/wiki/Dana_Rohrabacher" title="Dana Rohrabacher">Dana Rohrabacher</a> and others added that instead, an orbital  <a href="/wiki/Propellant_depot" title="Propellant depot">propellant depot</a> should be developed and the <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Crew_Development" title="Commercial Crew Development">Commercial Crew Development</a> program accelerated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SASRelease_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-SASRelease-125">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TSRHeavy_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-TSRHeavy-131">[130]</a></sup> Two studies, one not publicly released from NASA<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> and another from the <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Institute_of_Technology" title="Georgia Institute of Technology">Georgia Institute of Technology</a>, show this option to be possibly cheaper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Others suggest it will cost less to use an existing lower payload capacity rocket (<a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delta_IV" title="Delta IV">Delta IV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Falcon_9" title="Falcon 9">Falcon 9</a>, or the derivative <a href="/wiki/Falcon_Heavy" title="Falcon Heavy">Falcon Heavy</a>), with on-orbit assembly and propellant depots as needed, rather than develop a new launch vehicle for space exploration without competition for the whole design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ULAArchitecture_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-ULAArchitecture-136">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Review_of_United_States_Human_Space_Flight_Plans_Committee" title="Review of United States Human Space Flight Plans Committee">Augustine commission</a> proposed an option for a commercial 75&#160;metric ton launcher with lower operating costs, and noted that a 40 to 60&#160;t launcher can support lunar exploration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Committee_Final_Report_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Committee_Final_Report-141">[140]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mars_Society" title="Mars Society">Mars Society</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Robert_Zubrin" title="Robert Zubrin">Robert Zubrin</a>, who co-authored the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Direct" title="Mars Direct">Mars Direct</a> concept, suggested that a <a href="/wiki/Heavy-lift_launch_vehicle" title="Heavy-lift launch vehicle">heavy lift vehicle</a> should be developed for $5 billion on fixed-price requests for proposal. Zubrin also disagrees with those that say the U.S. does not need a heavy-lift vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a>\'s CEO <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk" title="Elon Musk">Elon Musk</a> stated in 2010 that he would "personally guarantee" that his company could build a launch vehicle in the 140&#8211;150&#160;t payload range, for $2.5 billion, or $300 million per launch, but cautioned that this price tag did not include a potential <a href="/wiki/Multistage_rocket" title="Multistage rocket">upper-stage</a> upgrade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup> Musk went on to start development of the fully reusable <a href="/wiki/BFR_(rocket)" title="BFR (rocket)">BFR</a> super-heavy launcher and upper stage <i>Starship</i> in the early 2010s. Because of this reusability, Musk claims that it will be the lowest cost <a href="/wiki/Super_heavy-lift_launch_vehicle" title="Super heavy-lift launch vehicle">super-heavy launcher</a> ever made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup> If the price per launch and payload capabilities for the BFR are anywhere near Musk\'s claimed capabilities, the rocket will be substantially cheaper than the SLS.\n</p><p>Rep. <a href="/wiki/Tom_McClintock" title="Tom McClintock">Tom McClintock</a> and other groups argue that the Congressional mandates forcing NASA to use Space Shuttle components for SLS amounts to a <a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a> non-competitive, single source requirement assuring contracts to existing shuttle suppliers, and calling the <a href="/wiki/Government_Accountability_Office" title="Government Accountability Office">Government Accountability Office</a> (GAO) to investigate possible violations of the <a href="/wiki/Competition_in_Contracting_Act" title="Competition in Contracting Act">Competition in Contracting Act</a> (CICA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFF_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-SFF-126">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SenateLS_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-SenateLS-147">[146]</a></sup> Opponents of the heavy launch vehicle have critically used the name "Senate launch system".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FI_DCSS_selected_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FI_DCSS_selected-62">[61]</a></sup>  The Competitive Space Task Force, in September 2011, said that the new government launcher directly violates NASA\'s charter, the Space Act, and the 1998 Commercial Space Act requirements for NASA to pursue the "fullest possible engagement of commercial providers" and to "seek and encourage, to the maximum extent possible, the fullest commercial use of space".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SASRelease_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-SASRelease-125">[124]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2013, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christopher_C._Kraft,_Jr." title="Christopher C. Kraft, Jr.">Chris Kraft</a>, the NASA mission control leader from the Apollo era, expressed his criticism of the system as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kraft_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kraft-148">[147]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lori_Garver" title="Lori Garver">Lori Garver</a>, former NASA Deputy Administrator, has called for cancelling the program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garver1_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garver1-149">[148]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Phil_Plait" title="Phil Plait">Phil Plait</a> has voiced his criticism of SLS in light of ongoing budget tradeoffs between <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Crew_Development" title="Commercial Crew Development">Commercial Crew Development</a> and SLS budget, also referring to earlier critique by Garver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plait1_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plait1-150">[149]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Doubts have also been expressed about the utility and cost of depots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup> "Patrick R. Chai and Alan W. Wilhite of Georgia Tech presented a study early in 2011 estimating that depot tanks would lose about $12 million worth of propellant per month in low Earth orbit if protected only with passive insulation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:PCR" title="Wikipedia:PCR"><span title="The material in the vicinity of this tag may lack sufficient context (March 2018)">context?</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/The_Planetary_Society" title="The Planetary Society">The Planetary Society</a> accepted that a Mars mission could be had with existing budgets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Missions">Missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
